<div id="review_2894971157" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2894971157" />
    <a title="Lauren " class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1291915-lauren"><img alt="Lauren " src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1604235645p2/1291915.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2894971157?book_show_action=true">Jul 29, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Lauren " class="user" itemprop="url" name="Lauren " href="/user/show/1291915-lauren">Lauren </a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1291915-lauren?shelf=own">own</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1291915-lauren?shelf=rtw21">rtw21</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1291915-lauren?shelf=translated-works">translated-works</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1291915-lauren?shelf=best-of-2020">best-of-2020</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1291915-lauren?shelf=scifi-speculative">scifi-speculative</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1291915-lauren?shelf=eastern-europe-lit">eastern-europe-lit</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2894971157" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6788831743432621252"><i>"Well, that's just the story... It all started when I was pearl-fishing on Tana Masa", the captain checked himself. "Or somewhere in those parts. Ah yes, it was some other island, but that's still my secret, old chap..."</i><br/><br/>From WAR WITH THE NEWTS by Karel Čapek, tr. from the Czech by Ewald Osers, 1936 Cz / 1999 En.<br/><br/>#ReadtheWorld21 📍Czechia<br/><br/>Delightful and creative satire - I laughed so much while reading this book. Not only is this story creative, but the form of the writing - parentheses, copious foo</span>
  <span id="freeText6788831743432621252" style="display:none"><i>"Well, that's just the story... It all started when I was pearl-fishing on Tana Masa", the captain checked himself. "Or somewhere in those parts. Ah yes, it was some other island, but that's still my secret, old chap..."</i><br /><br />From WAR WITH THE NEWTS by Karel Čapek, tr. from the Czech by Ewald Osers, 1936 Cz / 1999 En.<br /><br />#ReadtheWorld21 📍Czechia<br /><br />Delightful and creative satire - I laughed so much while reading this book. Not only is this story creative, but the form of the writing - parentheses, copious footnotes, news clippings and images - is so inventive and modern. Written in 1936, this is absurdity and satire to the highest degree, and it was a joy to read and experience.<br /><br />A Dutch boat captain encounters a human-sized aquatic species on an uninhabited Southeast Asian island. His industrious mind begins to imagine a trained work force of these newts. He procures financial backing from a wealthy Czech merchant to take the species to farm / exploit them for underwater labour... and in a few short years, society, and the earth itself looks a lot different.<br /><br />The early chapters of Book 1 discuss human and newt interactions, including a particularly hilarious chapter with a Hollywood starlet and a version of the monster genre film. The book goes on to satirize the science of the newts, their anatomy, brain, and their capacity for learning. There's a particularly hilarious interchange in a zoo where a captive newt begins talking to the zookeeper 😂<br /><br />Book 2 is the deepest dive and longest - and the one where I laughed the most! "Up the Ladder of Civilization" documents the evolution of the newts through news clippings, interviews, and academic articles. Book 3 is the "War" of the title. A showdown of newt and human... Which isn't really a war because by this time, newts outnumber humans 10 to 1. The last chapter is a very amusing meta turn, the author's internal dialogue questioning how to end the book.<br /><br />It is abundantly clear that this book was written in 1936, even at this pre-WWII time period, much of it pokes fun at Nazis, and German race science. Race and ethnicity and discussed and satirized frequently here, through the veil of the newts. Southeast Asian and African newts vs. Nordic newts. Parochialism, sexism, racism - e.g. some news clipping references to newts in America being lynched for their sexual advances on white women. Čapek wasn't holding back or hiding his political leanings here.<br /><br />Clear forebearer to the dark humor and satire of Kurt Vonnegut a few decades later. KV even blurbed this edition thanking the publisher for making this available to an English speaking audience.<br /><br />Čapek, who died in 1938, was a well-known and recognized writer in the interwar years, but unfortunately is not as well known today. This is my second work by him, following last year's read of his fantastic 1920 play, R.U.R., which was the first time the word "ROBOT" was coined. The themes of R.U.R. and WAR WITH THE NEWTS are very similar, and were both so enjoyable to read.<br /><br />📚 A brilliant satire of colonialism and nationalism - poking fun at all the establish institutions. Čapek's writing is brilliant, as is Osers' translation that doesn't miss a beat in the humor. Highly recommended.</span>
  <a data-text-id="6788831743432621252" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_1090039004" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1090039004" />
    <a title="Czarny Pies" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/29160274-czarny-pies"><img alt="Czarny Pies" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1400687408p2/29160274.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1090039004?book_show_action=true">Oct 26, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Czarny Pies" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Czarny Pies" href="/user/show/29160274-czarny-pies">Czarny Pies</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>


          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">Anyone wishing to understand how America armed ISIS.</span></div>

          <div>
    <span class="greyText">Recommended to Czarny by: </span>
      Lucie Nowak
  </div>


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/29160274-czarny-pies?shelf=czech-lit">czech-lit</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1090039004" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15118962489933958122">Save The War with the Newts for a time when you need a laugh. It will keep you in stitches from beginning to end. Once the laughing is over, you can add it to your "read" list on Good Reads on your "Great Classics of European Literature" and "masterpieces of satire" shelves. The newt is a remarkable fable that seems to describe the conflict for colonies that France, England and Germany waged in Africa in the second half of the nineteenth century and the bloody wars they fought on European soil i</span>
  <span id="freeText15118962489933958122" style="display:none">Save The War with the Newts for a time when you need a laugh. It will keep you in stitches from beginning to end. Once the laughing is over, you can add it to your "read" list on Good Reads on your "Great Classics of European Literature" and "masterpieces of satire" shelves. The newt is a remarkable fable that seems to describe the conflict for colonies that France, England and Germany waged in Africa in the second half of the nineteenth century and the bloody wars they fought on European soil in the twentieth century.<br /><br />While following this one major narrative, Capek finds time to shoot his comic darts at everything that moves: Fascism, international economics, government obfuscation, corporate cupidity, white supremacy, inhumane medical experiments, the arms industry, colonialism, the League of Nations, socialism, sexual relations, capitalism, etc. The main unifying theme is similar to that of Capek's R.U.R. which is that humans will ignore problems until they solve themselves by blowing up and bringing humanity back to ground zero.<br /><br />The basic plot spans roughly half a century from the discovery of a species of four-foot talking four-foot newts in a remote island in Dutch Indonesia. The newts are highly intelligent and compulsive workers. Consequently, they are enslaved and put to work on projects that make money for their owners. Other nations that do not wish to be left behind buy their own newts which reproducing via eggs and tadpoles increase very rapidly in number. Soon all countries with coasts have large numbers of newts off shore producing goods for them. The competition between nations switches to the military level so the newts are armed. At this point, the newts decide that they need more lebensraum (living space) so they use the explosives that they have been given to set off volcanic explosions that submerge most of the dry land on earth. The human race retreats to the alps and other mountainous areas. The author speculates that the Newts will eventually go to war with each other and wipe themselves out. The land will rise again and the humans can start to repopulate a new world.<br /><br />Although the parallel between the European competition for colonies in nineteenth century that was followed by the world wars in the twentieth century is unmistakeable, the War of the Newts has a message that stands apart from the specific historical context that inspired it. It is about the human instinct to do something that is profitable (e.g. colonialism) but resolutely ignoring the negative side (e.g. the enslavement of African workers).<br /><br />There is something for everyone in this book. My favourite part was where the Czechs protested that their culture was being submerged by the rapid growth of the world newt population. Because Czechoslovakia did not have a coast it could build its own Czech speaking populations. Moreover, the newts seemed to prefer standard English. This observation reminded me of the dilemma that we French speaking Canadians face. All the newcomers to our country want to speak English and so we are becoming a progressively smaller minority in our own country. Mort aux salamander!!<br /><br />American readers will be struck by how the human countries arm the newts (as the US armed the Taliban and various factions in liberated Iraq) and then were able to control what these groups did with them. Two wars at least are underway because of this practice. Capek saw it all.</span>
  <a data-text-id="15118962489933958122" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_3085409309" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3085409309" />
    <a title="Michael Perkins" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/13298726-michael-perkins"><img alt="Michael Perkins" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1454487234p2/13298726.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3085409309?book_show_action=true">Jan 03, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Michael Perkins" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Michael Perkins" href="/user/show/13298726-michael-perkins">Michael Perkins</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780425031681" href="/book/show/977383.War_with_the_Newts">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3085409309" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer531821758594477939">Author Karel Čapek was a contemporary of fellow Bohemians Franz Kafka and Jaroslav Hašek (author of The Good Soldier Švejk). During that time Prague was going through a period of cultural growth in literature, theater, art, and politics. <br/><br/>In 1921, Čapek's play, R.U.R. (“Rossum's Universal Robots") introduced the word and concept of the robot. The play depicted the conflict between these “synthetic people” and the human race. It also portrayed Čapek’s concern that the greatest threat to the human </span>
  <span id="freeText531821758594477939" style="display:none">Author Karel Čapek was a contemporary of fellow Bohemians Franz Kafka and Jaroslav Hašek (author of The Good Soldier Švejk). During that time Prague was going through a period of cultural growth in literature, theater, art, and politics. <br /><br />In 1921, Čapek's play, R.U.R. (“Rossum's Universal Robots") introduced the word and concept of the robot. The play depicted the conflict between these “synthetic people” and the human race. It also portrayed Čapek’s concern that the greatest threat to the human race was uncontrolled Faustian desire for power. <br /><br />This is the same theme of The War with the Newts (1936). Of the Newts, Čapek said: “You musn’t think that the evolution that gave rise to us was the only evolutionary possibility on the planet.”<br /><br />But he also wondered “would such a species fight the same wars and invite the same historical calamities as humans?” (By the end of the novel we find out. For one thing, the Chief Newt shows some of the same attributes of Hitler). <br /><br />Čapek’s felt his contemporaries were increasingly taken in by the false gods of technology and the heaping up of material goods; that they were running blind in pursuit of profits, amusement and pleasure. (Sound familiar?)<br /><br />================<br /><br />Thus is the background to Čapek’s satire, that begins with the discovery in the Pacific of a sea-dwelling race, an intelligent breed of newts, in what the locals fearfully refer to as Devil’s Bay, not far from Sumatra.<br /><br />The captain of the ship that arrives there soon recognizes the ability of the newts to gather oysters from the seabeds of the Bay, even as they fend off sharks. But the newts cannot open the shells. That’s left to the humans. Thus begins the exploitation of the newts for human gain.<br /><br />Eventually, an ambitious tycoon, Mr. Bondy, founds the Salamander Syndicate to exploit the newts in numerous ways as slave labor.<br /><br />Along the way, Čapek takes shots at these tycoons and their brainless starlet girlfriends, as well as racist Europeans and smug scientists.<br /><br />The newts rebel against their slavery and oppression and go to war with humans. The newts declare the need for more living space (what the Nazis called Lebensraum). The newts want more water and coastlines, and less landmass. This is an obvious threat to humans. <br /><br />Overarching the story is Čapek's anticipation of a new war among the European powers and the invasion of Czechoslovakia. He satire showed what was coming.<br /><br />==========<br /><br />Copies of this novel can be hard to come by. I discovered that Project Gutenberg, Australia, provides an online version.<br /><br /><a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="http://gutenberg.net.au/ebooks06/0601981h.html">http://gutenberg.net.au/ebooks06/0601...</a></span>
  <a data-text-id="531821758594477939" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/653230?book_show_action=true">Apr 09, 2007</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Ryan" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Ryan" href="/user/show/54609-ryan">Ryan</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780810114685" href="/book/show/33411.War_with_the_Newts">review of another edition</a>

          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">all</span></div>

        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/54609-ryan?shelf=done-reading">done-reading</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer653230" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer7605476101694315006">Synopsis: Kapek wrote in the post-WW1 era. In the book, a sea captain finds a bay that harbors giant newts, about the size of a ten year old human, complete with digits and all. The newts are intelligent, and he trains them to capture pearls for him. Eventually, a company forms that sends the Newts to all parts of the world for slave labor. Because of their intelligence (about equal to humans) and the training and supplies given them by man, the Newts spread all over the world (they get up to 70</span>
  <span id="freeText7605476101694315006" style="display:none">Synopsis: Kapek wrote in the post-WW1 era. In the book, a sea captain finds a bay that harbors giant newts, about the size of a ten year old human, complete with digits and all. The newts are intelligent, and he trains them to capture pearls for him. Eventually, a company forms that sends the Newts to all parts of the world for slave labor. Because of their intelligence (about equal to humans) and the training and supplies given them by man, the Newts spread all over the world (they get up to 70 billion) and eventually reach a position in which they can challenge mankind for supremacy of the Earth.<br /><br />My review: Kapek is one of the most informed, intelligent authors I have ever read. He discusses political and economic theory, scientific research (particularly evolution), social and moral movements in various states, racism and religious dogmatism, language development theories, new technologies - all with convincing and authoritative rhetoric. For the most part, he employs satire for the discussions, creating speeches and reports which the reader can't help but laugh at but which also produce a slight shudder (it is remarkably relevant today - particularly the denunciations of an unrestricted free-market and international relations / patriotism). Throughout the whole thing, I oscillated between laughter, scoffing, and awe, as I could not help but feel that I was reading the work of a gifted, concerned prophet.</span>
  <a data-text-id="7605476101694315006" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_76353591" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/76353591" />
    <a title="Lorenzo Berardi" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/183865-lorenzo-berardi"><img alt="Lorenzo Berardi" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1417557095p2/183865.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/76353591?book_show_action=true">Nov 01, 2009</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Lorenzo Berardi" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Lorenzo Berardi" href="/user/show/183865-lorenzo-berardi">Lorenzo Berardi</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780141192703" href="/book/show/7575624-war-with-the-newts">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/183865-lorenzo-berardi?shelf=a-my-english-library">a-my-english-library</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/183865-lorenzo-berardi?shelf=2011">2011</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/183865-lorenzo-berardi?shelf=czech">czech</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer76353591" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4955860495950874122">VÁLKA S MLOKY: A RECIPE<br/><br/>It may not be a conventional Czech or Slovakian speciality, but a válka s mloky is an excellent and tasty alternative to the unbearable lightness of being when a metamorphosis into an engineer of the human souls is too loud a solitude.<br/><br/>Preparation time: 1936-1937<br/>Cooking time: approximately 3 days<br/><br/>You will need:<br/>- An aquarium<br/>- An atlas reporting the pre World War II borders<br/>- Around 100 newts of both genders<br/>- Sea salt <br/>- Lemon<br/>- Mayonnaise<br/>- Biscuits in crumbs<br/>- Granny Smith apples</span>
  <span id="freeText4955860495950874122" style="display:none">VÁLKA S MLOKY: A RECIPE<br /><br />It may not be a conventional Czech or Slovakian speciality, but a válka s mloky is an excellent and tasty alternative to the unbearable lightness of being when a metamorphosis into an engineer of the human souls is too loud a solitude.<br /><br />Preparation time: 1936-1937<br />Cooking time: approximately 3 days<br /><br />You will need:<br />- An aquarium<br />- An atlas reporting the pre World War II borders<br />- Around 100 newts of both genders<br />- Sea salt <br />- Lemon<br />- Mayonnaise<br />- Biscuits in crumbs<br />- Granny Smith apples<br />- A long spoon<br />- A dictionary<br />- A wireless radio<br />- A comfortable armchair<br />- Several bottles of Pilsner Urquell<br />- Small explosive devices.<br /><br />Method:<br />Fill the aquarium with tap water. Put sea salt into it. Mix it up with the long spoon. <br />Uncork a Pilsner Urquell. Pour a few drops of beer into the salty water. Mix again in clockwise circles. <br />Take your time. Move the armchair close to the aquarius so that everything is at hand's reach. <br />When the daylight is fading out, turn on a lampshade.<br /><br />Start adding up pinches of fresh newts into the aquarium. Let them swim creating their sexual milieu. Let the newts multiply. When the newts reach a number of thousands, switch on the radio and find some station broadcasting a selection of classic music. Then, open the dictionary and read in a clear, firm and loud voice all the terms between letters A and M and let the newts repeat them.<br />(You can have some breaks for uncorking additional bottles of Pilsner Urquell).<br /><br />Feed the newts with the biscuits crumbs. Add lemon at will. Turn off the light. Switch off the radio. Go to sleep. Shut the door of your bedroom. Let the newts multiply and explore your living room mainland out of their aquarius during the night time. <br /><br />The morning after start by mixing up the aquarium waters clockwise with the long spoon. Your newts won't bother. Switch on the radio looking for some jazz or, even better, either charleston or fox-trot. Read in a clear, firm and loud voice all the terms between letters N and Z in the dictionary. Let the newts repeat them. <br />At tea-time feed the newts with more biscuits crumbs and give them some slices of Granny Smith apples.<br /><br />Take the atlas and look for the following items: <br />1. A map showing Europe in the 1930s. <br />2. A map showing Indonesia. <br />3. A map of the United States focusing on Louisiana. <br />4. A map of Japan. <br />5. A map of Africa including Cape Verde. <br /><br />Put the atlas in a way so that its open pages are facing the long side of the aquarium. When the <br />night falls, turn off the light but don't switch off the radio. Leave the small explosive devices not too far from the aquarium, at newt hands' reach. <br />Shut the door of your bedroom and lock yourself in. Some sleep pills may help. <br /><br />At the dawn of the third day, your válka s mloky will be finally ready. You could serve millions of educated newts with mayonnaise and Granny Smith apples if you like. Now two problems may rise while coping with this elaborate Czech-made delicacy. <br /><br />First and foremost, it must be said that newts are not really edible. But this unforeseen difficulty can be easily solved by boiling them up (if you will manage to convince them) so that your newts will taste of inferior beef. <br />Secondly, your living room is likely to be already underwater by now while the rest of your home, armchair and radio included, has been blown up by those small explosive devices the newts are so fond of. <br />No worries: you can work it out. With the newts. For the sake of the newts.</span>
  <a data-text-id="4955860495950874122" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_1369970258" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1369970258" />
    <a title="sologdin" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5610133-sologdin"><img alt="sologdin" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1307928658p2/5610133.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1369970258?book_show_action=true">Aug 21, 2015</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="sologdin" class="user" itemprop="url" name="sologdin" href="/user/show/5610133-sologdin">sologdin</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5610133-sologdin?shelf=burns-like-cold-iron">burns-like-cold-iron</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5610133-sologdin?shelf=blew-it-up-you-maniacs">blew-it-up-you-maniacs</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1369970258" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17881427881959052889">Capitalists seeking downward pressure on wages create their antithesis in the form of effective slave labor force that very predictably revolts and supersedes them.<br/><br/>First third is episodic, almost low farce. We know the poor newts are in for it when the guy who ‘discovers’ them refers casually to “nigger superstitions” (12) and “realized that the honor of the white race was now at stake” (14). Discoverer goes to investor in order to obtain backing for his plan to exploit newt labor, which investo</span>
  <span id="freeText17881427881959052889" style="display:none">Capitalists seeking downward pressure on wages create their antithesis in the form of effective slave labor force that very predictably revolts and supersedes them.<br /><br />First third is episodic, almost low farce. We know the poor newts are in for it when the guy who ‘discovers’ them refers casually to “nigger superstitions” (12) and “realized that the honor of the white race was now at stake” (14). Discoverer goes to investor in order to obtain backing for his plan to exploit newt labor, which investor, in listening to his pitch, adopts the standard bourgeois mystical-aesthetic self-assessment: “What a bore! He’ll be telling me he could ship sewing machines to Tasmania or steam boilers and pins to Fiji. Terrific deal, I know. That’s all I’m good to you for. To hell, I’m no shopkeeper. I’m a visionary. I’m a poet” (32). Whatever asshole; you'll sell at a half-cent profit today the rope with which I hang you tomorrow.<br /><br />For the US, the rightwing protested the newts, “and a number of negroes were either hanged or burnt to death” (73). This is a recurrent theme: <blockquote>In the American press, reports cropped up from time to time of girls who claimed to have been raped by Newts while bathing. In consequence, there occurred increasingly frequent instances in the United States of Newts being caught and lynched, mostly by burning at the stake. In vain did scientists protest against this popular custom by pointing out that on anatomical grounds such an offence on the part of salamanders was physically impossible. […] At that time the movement against Newt Lynching came into being, led by the Negro Rev. Robert J. Washington; this soon gained about a hundred thousand members, though almost exclusively among the Negro population. The American press began to claim that this movement was political and subversive; in consequence raids were made on black residential areas and a lot of Negroes were burnt to death while they were praying in their churches for their brothers, the Newts. (154)</blockquote> Importantly, this bit is in a footnote, and author concludes that “this belongs only marginally to the history of the Newts” (id.), which should mark it out as the moment of the text’s deconstruction.<br /><br />Satire proceeds also in the normal swiftian manner (see for instance the list of experiments at 136 ff.), insofar as scientific endeavor is often presented as mere vanity; more importantly, though <blockquote>eventually the natural history of every nation had its own giant salamanders and was waging a furious scientific war against the giant salamanders of other nations. (78) </blockquote> That is, scientific ‘knowledge’ is presented as always already political. One report found that the newts “can read, though only the evening papers” (85), noting coincidentally that the newt “is interested in the same things as the average Englishman and reacts to them in a similar way” (id.)—which is to suggest the formative role of the bourgeois press in consciousness production.<br /><br />First portion develops ultimately to the foundation of the ‘salamander syndicate,’ wherein a bunch of cappies have a board meeting as to the trajectory of the newt business. Breeding newts for forced labor is presented as the condition of possibility for “entering upon Utopia” (106). <br /><br />Interlude before Act II is an appendix, “The Sex Life of Newts,” wherein it is revealed that the salamanders reproduce via “sexual milieu” (110) triggered by emissions of the males, which emissions are themselves not carriers of genetic materials. This allows “some kind of inherited illusion of paternity” (id.), arising out of “a strange ritual called the salamander dance” (112), “one of the great erotic illusions and it is compensated for, in a most interesting manner, by just these great male festivities”; the dance “overcomes that atavistic and senseless illusion of male sexual individualism.” This all leads to a “strange Newt collectivism” in which “the females play such a subordinate part” (113)—this produces “the establishment of male collectivity and solidarity.” <br /><br />Second part is conceptually very cool, as it is a historical essay constructed out of newspaper clippings (lotsa great fake footnotes). Initially, “conservative circles strongly objected to this introduction of a new workforce on the grounds that it represented unfair competition with human labour” (123). Newt trade very quickly becomes “a modern form of piracy” (132), <i>hostis humani generis</i>, but nevertheless “now tacitly tolerated” (133). Eventually, after much use, <blockquote>people began to regard the Newts as something just as natural as a calculating machine or some other mechanical gadget; they no longer considered them something mysterious that had risen from the depths of the sea, heaven only knew why and what for. (138)</blockquote> This indicates that science banished mysticism as it concerned the salamanders—which is fairly plainly a rebuke the irrational presentation of reptilians that one finds in Lovecraft (or in Icke).<br /><br />Second section marks out its own dialectical development to the extent that “the moment the Newts had become a mass-scale and commonplace phenomenon the whole question of the Newts, as we may so call it, underwent a change” (141), whereupon, because “the protagonist of the Newt problem—as so often before in the history of human progress—was, of course, women” (142), some sought “a proper education for the Newts” who are otherwise noted to have “an insatiable thirst for knowledge”(140). Educational policy disputes were “eventually solved by the salamander schools being taken over by the state and the schools for young humans being reformed with a view to bringing them as near as possible in line with the Reformed School for Newts” (144). Newt education leads inter alia to “the first Newt was subsequently to be awarded the degree of Doctor of Law” (id.). <br /><br />It therefore was “easy enough to fit the salamanders into, as it was called, the production process” but more “difficult to fit them, somehow or other, into the existing social order” (151). <blockquote>The more conservative of the population denied the existence of any legal or public problems: the Newts were simply the property of their employers who were responsible for them and who were liable for any damage that might be caused by the Newts. In spite of their undeniable intelligence (these people argued) the salamanders were no more than a legal object, a chattel or an item or property, and any special legislative arrangements concerning the Newts would be interference with the sacred right of private ownership. (151-52)</blockquote><br />Though the French left wing wanted a welfare statute on behalf of the Newts, “the extreme Left, by contrast, demanded that the Newts be expelled as enemies of the working people: in the service of capitalism they were working too hard and for virtually nothing” (153). That there is fairly ugly, I think, and not an accurate summary of a leftwing position (it is more a fascist position); it does expose perhaps that the leftwing may be anthropocentrist. Italy by contrast (presumably under il Duce) formed “a special Newt Corporation, composed of employers and authorities” (153), which is the normal way to establish cross-class loyalties in order to displace Marxist class antagonism. (“In short, each country tackled its Newt problem in its own particular way.”) Consistent therewith, “the first truly international and fundamental acknowledgment of Newt nationhood came in that famous manifesto of the Communist International signed by Comrade Molokov and addressed to ‘all oppressed and revolutionary Newts of the whole world’” (157-58). So, there we go. ‘Comrade Molokov’ is a slick little joke, as text had just prior thereto noted that some newts took up “adoration of Moloch” (157) because “the name Moloch reminded them of the zoological <i>molche</i> or the German <i>Molch</i>, the terms for Newt” (id.). <br /><br />Third section chronicles the run-up to, prosecution of, and conclusion to the war. Like Thucydides’ presentation of various incidents leading up to the war, author here presents several precursor massacres. One such incident is Germania claiming that “we need new space for our Newts” (193), <i>lebensraum</i> of a sort. Various warnings are sounded regarding the decline of humans (197 et seq.) and the necessity of a “populist anti-newt movement” (208). Newts take initiative by sending an earthquake to New Orleans and vicinity, then to China, then to Africa—followed by a great little moment that must be the basis for Ayn Rand’s ludicrous little ‘This is John Galt speaking’ speech: <blockquote>radio hams throughout most of Europe observed heavy interference on their receivers, just as if some new, unusually powerful transmitter had gone into operation. […] ‘Hello, hello, hello! The Chief salamander speaking. Hello, the Chief Salamander speaking. Stop all broadcasting, you men. […] Hello, you humans! Louisiana calling. Kiangsu calling. Senegambia calling. We regret the loss of human lives. We do not wish to inflict unnecessary losses on you. We only want you to evacuate the seashores in the places we shall notify you of from time to time. […] There’s no longer room for us on your continents. That’s why we have to dismantle your continents. […] You wanted us. You spread us all over the globe. Now you’ve got us. (215-16)</blockquote><br />Various metafictional codas thereafter. Good stuff. <br /><br />Recommended for those would not offend human feelings, readers never before closer to perpetual peace, and violators of ancient, pure and original Newtdom. <br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="17881427881959052889" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

<div id="review_2672529456" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2672529456" />
    <a title="Phil Jensen" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/14413650-phil-jensen"><img alt="Phil Jensen" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1357693002p2/14413650.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2672529456?book_show_action=true">Jan 13, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Phil Jensen" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Phil Jensen" href="/user/show/14413650-phil-jensen">Phil Jensen</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2672529456" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer16889935547291788045"><b>What you need to know about this great book</b><br/><br/>Part 1:<br/><br/>Our story begins with the fascinating, yet verbosely racist Captain Van Toch. You, as the reader, are meant to laugh at this man's foibles, all-encompassing prejudices, and larger than life character. He's a bit like Sancho Panza in his blind dedication to complete folly.<br/><br/>The story begins with a narrow scope- one trip by one captain, and gradually builds up to the ramifications on international trade, as discussed by a board of directors.<br/><br/>This book</span>
  <span id="freeText16889935547291788045" style="display:none"><b>What you need to know about this great book</b><br /><br />Part 1:<br /><br />Our story begins with the fascinating, yet verbosely racist Captain Van Toch. You, as the reader, are meant to laugh at this man's foibles, all-encompassing prejudices, and larger than life character. He's a bit like Sancho Panza in his blind dedication to complete folly.<br /><br />The story begins with a narrow scope- one trip by one captain, and gradually builds up to the ramifications on international trade, as discussed by a board of directors.<br /><br />This book is widely lauded as satire, but I suggest that it is much more. Portions of Part 1 function more as hypothetical what-ifs, and the social commentary is buried, sometimes not too deeply under the what-if in the way that reminds me of an <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/880695.H_G__Wells" title="H.G. Wells" rel="nofollow noopener">H.G. Wells</a> story would.<br /><br />Part 2:<br /><br />This part consists almost entirely of newspaper clippings describing a series of social, technological, scientific, political, and economic developments. It is many things: satire of the world's reactions to new discoveries and the folly of misdirected intellectualism; commentary on hypocrisy and selfishness; convincing world-building; expert parody of many styles of writing, and slow-burning suspense. It is clear to the reader that this is not going to end well, but humanity continues hurtling willy-nilly down the path to destruction.<br /><br />Part 3:<br /><br />This part slows down the pace from Part 2 and describes the inevitable clash between species. It is not a pitched battle. It is a confluence of politics, economics, and technology. It is also a pointed commentary on appeasement- countries are willing to sacrifice each other as long as the enemy is at someone else's gate.<br /><br />Special comments on the last few chapters: <br /><br />*Spoiler city<br /><br />Capek brilliantly returns to the feel of the beginning of the novel- a lonely old man in a boat, and a narrow focus on one journey. There is a powerful sense of pathos when the butler finally realizes that he has been wrong all along, and the newts are coming for his home. It was a bold move for Capek to append an unironically tragic ending to an often satirical book.<br /><br />So how does the book end? My take: the newts win. Every review I've read endorses the what if scenario of biological warfare in the last chapter, but to my reading that was clearly sarcastic. <br /><br />The last chapter is an interview between the "author" and the "writer." I assume they are both Capek. One party insists that this is the obvious ending- the newts win and humanity becomes a servant race. It is the obvious outcome of humanity's short-sightedness and hypocrisy. Everyone has acted according to their nature, and this is the natural end. The other party insists that that is too sad an ending, and begs for a happier one. The first party says, fine, have your bedtime story, the newts kill each other off. Happy now?<br /><br />Only we should not be happy now, because that ending is not the truth- just what we would rather believe.</span>
  <a data-text-id="16889935547291788045" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_18650592" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/18650592" />
    <a title="Robert" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1024257-robert"><img alt="Robert" src="https://s.gr-assets.com/assets/nophoto/user/m_50x66-82093808bca726cb3249a493fbd3bd0f.png" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/18650592?book_show_action=true">Mar 25, 2008</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Robert" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Robert" href="/user/show/1024257-robert">Robert</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer18650592" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3571307354972732319">A brilliant book. This book is one of the high peaks between Swift and Twain and later broad scale social critics in fiction such as Kurt Vonnegut. Published in 1936 it takes it chops up a broad sample of contemporary politics, culture and values. Most of what it has to say about mankind and its collective arrogance holds up today with astounding clarity. This book has not dated at all in any significant way. <br/><br/>The book is about the discovery of an intelligent species of water dwelling beings appa</span>
  <span id="freeText3571307354972732319" style="display:none">A brilliant book. This book is one of the high peaks between Swift and Twain and later broad scale social critics in fiction such as Kurt Vonnegut. Published in 1936 it takes it chops up a broad sample of contemporary politics, culture and values. Most of what it has to say about mankind and its collective arrogance holds up today with astounding clarity. This book has not dated at all in any significant way. <br /><br />The book is about the discovery of an intelligent species of water dwelling beings apparently descended from salamanders (they can regenerate almost any part of their bodies), the human assisted propagation of their kind to the world's sea coasts, and an eventual war between mankind and the Newts (guess who doesn't win). The book is divided into three sub-books. The first concerns how the Newts were discovered by a adventurous sea captain on his relentless search for pearls, how he spread their settlements across the Pacific and Indian oceans, and involved a leading industrialist in the project. The second concerns events after the captain's passing when, having so successfully harvested pearls that the market bottoms out, the industrialist launches a program to globally exploit the Newts on a wide variety of programs mainly aimed at creating new land along the coasts. This part describes the ruthless means of exploitation employed by business, callous and cruel research performed by scientists, the antro-centric moralizing of religionists, the complicity of governments, and the use of Newts as weapons of war by those governments. The third recounts the war that begins when the Newts, now well educated by their human oppressors and well armed with the weapons and other technology supplied by governments and industrial explosives used in their undersea construction work, organize, resist and start to pursue projects to benefit their kind and its now burgeoning population. Mankind for its part fails to understand the real stakes in time, and gladly assists in bringing about its own doom since there has grown up a huge economy of trade in food, technology and explosives with the Newts that no government or corporation wishes to disrupt lest there be poor financial results or unemployment.<br /><br />One can find a clear image of almost any current day political, religious, economic or ecological issue here. But mainly one gets a a true image of the real enemy, the persistent antrocentrism of humanity and its view of nature and the cosmos reflected in our cultures, religions, economics and way of life, a trait so ingrained that it is hardly noticed but rather taken for granted and imbued in most quarters with virtue and a cherished inevitability.<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="3571307354972732319" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_2497309960" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2497309960" />
    <a title="Lemar" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5172572-lemar"><img alt="Lemar" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1595783002p2/5172572.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2497309960?book_show_action=true">Oct 03, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Lemar" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Lemar" href="/user/show/5172572-lemar">Lemar</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Kindle Edition " href="/book/show/27110955-war-with-the-newts">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5172572-lemar?shelf=speculative-fiction">speculative-fiction</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2497309960" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer7148382868676549771">Karl Capek is a master. Maybe because he came of age as a writer in a political environment where he had to get his work past censors, he is insanely sly and effective at using a story as a parable. The first trick is writing a story that is engaging in and of itself while providing a secondary layer for those readers who are looking for it. In the case of War with the Newts, a race of amphibians with human like qualities are discovered (in Chapter 1). This provides the basis for a great story. </span>
  <span id="freeText7148382868676549771" style="display:none">Karl Capek is a master. Maybe because he came of age as a writer in a political environment where he had to get his work past censors, he is insanely sly and effective at using a story as a parable. The first trick is writing a story that is engaging in and of itself while providing a secondary layer for those readers who are looking for it. In the case of War with the Newts, a race of amphibians with human like qualities are discovered (in Chapter 1). This provides the basis for a great story. But the subtext increases the fun exponentially. Sometimes it hides in plain sight like this, <br /> “Listen, demons don’t exist. And if they did exist they would look like Europeans.”<br /><br /> “An Irish mass is better. You see, in Ireland they’ve got these priests that really can work magic. Just like some fakir or pagan.”<br /><br />Keeping in mind that this was written in the early 1930s, Capek’s moral compass and use of stinging irony were groundbreaking.<br /> “in the states of the Midwest the Movement for the Suppression of Immorality gained enormously in numbers; there were public demonstrations and several Negroes were hanged or burned alive.”<br /><br />Capek’s roasting of business is hilarious and still dead on. <br /> “The same applies to the decline of the Roman Empire, the colonization of continents, the massacre of the Indians and so on. All of this could be completed incomparably faster if put into the hands of well funded business.” <br /><br /> The translator for the version I read was David Wyllie. There is a new translation out and I compared them; the old one just can’t be improved on in my opinion and costs 99 cents for the Kindle version. Wyllie just absolutely got Capek, I can’t recommend this highly enough. </span>
  <a data-text-id="7148382868676549771" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1290685498" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1290685498" />
    <a title="Aaron Gertler" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/7027368-aaron-gertler"><img alt="Aaron Gertler" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1503641184p2/7027368.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1290685498?book_show_action=true">May 26, 2015</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Aaron Gertler" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Aaron Gertler" href="/user/show/7027368-aaron-gertler">Aaron Gertler</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1290685498" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer12420036653923360388">This may be a "dystopian satire", but I read it neither as dystopia nor as satire. It's certainly a funny book, but it's not especially satirical: Instead, the author takes a very simple premise and drives it off the Cliffs of Insanity (in a good way). This could have been a ten-page short story, but instead it just spirals wonderfully out of control without ever becoming boring. <br/><br/>Also: The Newts are some of the most memorable aliens in the history of science fiction, which is amazing for a book </span>
  <span id="freeText12420036653923360388" style="display:none">This may be a "dystopian satire", but I read it neither as dystopia nor as satire. It's certainly a funny book, but it's not especially satirical: Instead, the author takes a very simple premise and drives it off the Cliffs of Insanity (in a good way). This could have been a ten-page short story, but instead it just spirals wonderfully out of control without ever becoming boring. <br /><br />Also: The Newts are some of the most memorable aliens in the history of science fiction, which is amazing for a book written before the term "science fiction" existed.</span>
  <a data-text-id="12420036653923360388" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('FWsNnFKyem2xtCdOdwt044Kieiia0r1PHOPrO0rPkvFqZlschY5pXzR+IFOBJ/p/+k/bwmtQyfcoyi9Cx9Pz9g==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

  function refreshGroupBox(group_id, book_id) {
    new Ajax.Updater('addGroupBooks' + book_id + '', '/group/add_book_box', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){refreshGroupBoxComplete(request, book_id);}, parameters:'id=' + group_id + '&book_id=' + book_id + '&refresh=true' + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('yD4KmkQ84bei/uEwzrBycAtap2ozvKYgoiJSUFIsS8e3M1wakwDyhSc05i04nPzsc7cGgMI+0piWC5Yp3zAqwA==')})
  }
//]]>
</script>

May, h<\/span>\n  <span id=\"freeText70866029082572045\" style=\"display:none\">Often compared to Byron, Keats, Shelley, and Poe, called Lautreamont\'s &quot;elder brother&quot; by the Czech Surrealists, Karel Hynek Mácha (1810-1836) was the greatest Czech Romantic poet, and arguably the most influential of any poet in the language. May, his epic masterpiece, was published in April 1836, just seven months before his death. Considered the &quot;pearl&quot; of Czech poetry, it is a tale of seduction, revenge, and patricide. A paean as well to nature, the beauty of its music and its innovative use of language, expertly captured in this new translation by Marcela Sulak, has ensured the poem\'s lasting popularity. Scorned at first by the national revivalists of the 19th century for being &quot;un-Czech,&quot; Mácha was held up as a &quot;national&quot; poet by later generations, a fate which the interwar Czech avant-garde, who considered him a precursor, took it upon themselves to reverse.\n\nUnlike the other seminal 19th-century European poets, Mácha\'s work has been largely ignored in English translation. The present volume, the only available in English, provides the original Czech text in parallel and includes a series of illustrations by Jindřich Štyrský specifically created for the poem.<\/span>\n Speaking to a group of sunbathing women who remind him of lovers past, this elderly roué tells the story of his life—or at least unburdens himself of a lifetime\'s worth of stories. Thus we learn of amatory conquests (and humiliations), of scandals both private and public, of military adventures and domestic feuds, of what things were like &quot;in the days of the monarchy&quot; and how they\'ve changed since. As the book tumbles restlessly forward, and the comic tone takes on darker shadings, we realize we are listening to a man talking as much out of desperation as from exuberance.\n\nHrabal, one of the great Czech writers of the twentieth century, as well as an inveterate haunter of Prague\'s pubs and football stadiums, developed a unique method which he termed &quot;palavering,&quot; whereby characters gab and soliloquize with abandon. Part drunken boast, part soul-rending confession, part metaphysical poem on the nature of love and time, this astonishing novel (which unfolds in a single monumental sentence) shows why he has earned Told from the point of view of a mother, three sisters, and the daughter of one of the sisters, this story of secrets and betrayals takes us from the daily rhythms of nomadic life on the steppe to the harsh realities of urban alcoholism and prostitution in the capital, Ulaanbaatar.       <div id="aboutAuthor" class=" clearFloats bigBox"><div class="h2Container gradientHeaderContainer"><h2 class="brownBackground"><a href="/author/show/439723.Karel_apek">About Karel Čapek</a></h2></div><div class="bigBoxBody"><div class="bigBoxContent containerWithHeaderContent">
          <div class='bookAuthorProfile'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__topContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__photoContainer'>
<a href="/author/show/439723.Karel_apek"><div class='bookAuthorProfile__photo' style='background-image: url(https://images.gr-assets.com/authors/1208185188p3/439723.jpg);'></div>
</a></div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__widgetContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__name'>
<a href="/author/show/439723.Karel_apek">Karel Čapek
</a></div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__followerCount'>
615
followers
</div>

</div>
</div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__about'>

<span id="freeTextContainer8171499431112060978">Karel Čapek is one of the the most influential Czech writers of the 20th century. He wrote with intelligence and humour on a wide variety of subjects. His works are known for their interesting and precise descriptions of reality, and Čapek is renowned for his excellent work with the Czech language. His play <i>R.U.R. (Rossum's Universal Robots)</i> first popularized the word "robot".</span>

</div>
</div>

<div class="clear"></div></div></div><div class="bigBoxBottom"></div></div>

      <div class=" clearFloats bigBox"><div class="h2Container gradientHeaderContainer"><h2 class="brownBackground"><a href="/work/quotes/1469484-v-lka-s-mloky">Quotes from War with the Newts</a></h2></div><div class="bigBoxBody"><div class="bigBoxContent containerWithHeaderContent">
          <div class="stacked" tyle="padding-bottom:15px">
            <span class="readable">&ldquo;It suddenly occurred to me that every move on the chessboard is old and has been played by somebody at some time. Maybe our own history has been played out by somebody at some time, and we just move our pieces about in the same moves to strike in the same way as people have always done.&rdquo;</span>
            <nobr>
              &mdash;
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/quotes/618192-it-suddenly-occurred-to-me-that-every-move-on-the">17 likes</a>
            </nobr>
            <div class="clear"></div>
          </div>
          <div class="stacked" tyle="padding-bottom:15px">
            <span class="readable">&ldquo;They all had a thousand good economic and political reasons why they couldn’t stop. I’m not a politician or a businessman; how am I supposed to persuade them about these things. What are we supposed to do; quite likely the world will collapse and disappear under water; but at least that will happen for political and economic reasons we can all understand, at least it will happen with the help of science, technology and public opinion, with human ingenuity of all sorts! Not some cosmic catastrophe but just the same old reasons to do with the struggle for power and money and so on. There’s nothing we can do about that.&rdquo;</span>
            <nobr>
              &mdash;
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/quotes/7213069-they-all-had-a-thousand-good-economic-and-political-reasons">14 likes</a>
            </nobr>
